The Proof That Inseparability from Originated Things Implies Origination
Proof that if distinctness and mixing are originated, the principles must also be originated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani argues that if the two principles (light and darkness) cannot exist without originated distinctness and mixing, then they themselves must be originated.

Core Idea
What never precedes the originated and is never free of it must itself be originated.

Imam al-Baqillani continues his refutation of dualism by examining the relationship between the two eternal principles and the attributes of distinctness and mixing. He forces the dualist to confront a dilemma: either the principles are inseparable from originated features, which would make them originated, or they must abandon their claim of pre-eternal distinctness. This passage sharpens the core kalam principle that anything eternally inseparable from the contingent is itself contingent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist's claims
The dualists assert that the two principles (light and darkness) are eternal and originally distinct. Then they differ on whether distinctness and mixing are eternal or originated.
2
Follow the first objection
If they say distinctness remains even when mixing occurs, they face a contradiction: the world would both be and not be simultaneously.
The author calls this ignorance.
3
Follow the second objection
If they say both distinctness and mixing are originated, ask: can the principles be free of them? If yes, they abandon eternal distinctness. If no, then the principles are inseparable from originated things.
4
Apply the general rule
The author applies the principle: whatever is never free of the originated and does not precede it is itself originated. Since light and darkness cannot precede distinctness and mixing, they must be originated.
This is a key argument against dualism.
5
Conclude the proof
Thus the dualist position is self-defeating: their eternal principles turn out to be originated, contradicting their own claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا بل التباين باق موجود في حالة وجود الامتزاج قيل لهم فيجب أن يكونا متباينين ممتزجين وأن تكون اليوم دنيا وألا تكون دنيا لوجود الامتزاج والتباين اللذين كانت لأحدهما ولم تكن للآخر وهذا جهل وإن قالوا إن تباين الأصلين محدث وامتزاجهما محدث قيل لهم فهل ينفك الأصلان من التباين والامتزاج فإن قالوا نعم تركوا قولهم بتباين الأصلين في القدم وإن قالوا لا قيل لهم فيجب القضاء على حدوث النور والظلام إذا كانا لا ينفكان من حادثين ولا يخلوان منهما وكنا قد بينا أن ما لم ينفك من المحدث ولم يسبقه فهو محدث
And if they say: rather, distinctness remains existing in the state of the existence of mixing, it is said to them: then they must be distinct and mixed, and that today it is a world and not a world because of the existence of mixing and distinctness, for which the world was because of one of them and not because of the other. This is ignorance. And if they say: the distinctness of the two principles is originated and their mixing is originated, it is said to them: are the two principles free of distinctness and mixing? If they say: yes, they abandon their statement about the distinctness of the two principles in pre-eternity. If they say: no, it is said to them: then it must be judged that light and darkness are originated, since they are not free of two originated things nor do they lack them. And we have already shown that whatever is not free of the originated and does not precede it is originated.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage continues the dialectic by presenting two alternative responses from the dualists. First, if they insist that distinctness persists even when mixing occurs, they face the contradiction that the world both is and is not at the same time, which is absurd. Second, if they claim that both distinctness and mixing are originated events, then the eternal principles (light and darkness) must be inseparable from these originated events. Since they cannot exist without them, and since these events are originated, the principles themselves must be originated. The author appeals to a previously established principle: anything that is never free of that which is originated and does not precede it is itself originated. Thus the dualist position collapses.

Key Terms
تباين
امتزاج
محدث
القديم
ينفك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that anything inseparable from originated things is originated without further condition.
Correction: The argument relies on the specific condition that the thing does not precede the originated. If something precedes the originated (like an eternal cause), it would not be originated. But here, the principles do not precede distinctness and mixing, so they are originated.

Checkpoint
What consequence follows if the two principles cannot be free of originated distinctness and mixing?
A) The principles are eternal.
B) The principles are originated.
C) The principles become one.
D) The world does not exist.

Key Takeaway
If something is never free of originated things and does not precede them, it must itself be originated.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief that, upon deeper examination, turned out to be logically inconsistent? Are you willing to abandon it even if it is comfortable?
?
The author uses a dilemma to force the dualist to choose between two impossible options. Is there an area of your own faith where you have avoided asking tough questions because the answers might be uncomfortable?
?
If you believe in a Creator, does your understanding of creation allow for anything to be eternally inseparable from change? Will you let this principle challenge how you think about the universe?

Refutation of Infinite Regress and the Meaning of 'Originated'
Refutation of infinite regress and logical definition of 'originated'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Baqillani refutes the claims of infinite regress and proves that originated events must have a beginning from non-existence.

Core Idea
Originated means 'from non-existence'—so it cannot have no beginning.

This passage presents two tightly woven arguments: first against the dualists who posit an endless cycle of distinctness and mixing, and second against materialists who claim originated events have no beginning. The logic hinges on the very definition of 'originated' (hadith) and exposes a self-contradiction in the opposing view.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualists' claim
Read the opening conditional: the dualists assert that distinctness and mixing alternate without beginning or end. Recognize that this is an infinite regress.
The term 'dualists' refers to those who believe in two eternal principles.
2
Refutation of infinite regress
The author points out that if mixing has no beginning, then in pre-eternity (al-azal) mixing must already have occurred, implying the world was eternal—contradicting the dualists' own claim that the world is originated.
This refutation shows the absurdity of an actual infinite regress of events.
3
Analyze the definition of 'originated'
The word 'originated' (hadith) means 'coming from non-existence' (min 'adam). Therefore, any collection of originated things must consist solely of things that began to exist.
This is a linguistic and conceptual analysis, not just a theological assertion.
4
Identify the self-contradiction
Materialists claim both that there is no beginning for originated things and that they are originated. But the second claim requires a beginning. Hence their position is impossible.
The author concludes they have no valid response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا لا غاية لتباين الأصلين وامتزاجهما وإن كانا حادثين ولا تباين إلا وقبله امتزاج ولا امتزاج إلا وقبله تباين أبدا لا أول لذلك ولا غاية قيل لهم فمحال على قولكم هذا أن الدنيا لم تكن في الأزل لأن الامتزاج على قولكم هذا لا أول له ولا شيء منه إلا وقد كان قبله مثله وتباين يخالفه إلى غير غاية ثم يقال لهم ولمن قال بذلك من أهل الدهر إن قولكم لا أول للحوادث يقتضي قدمها وأنها لم تكن عن عدم وقولكم فيها إنها حوادث نقض لذلك لأن القول حوادث هو جمع حادث والحادث حقيقته أنه ما عن عدم فحقيقة الجمع الذي يقع عليه الاسم أنه موجود عن عدم المحال أن يدخل في جمع الحوادث ما لا أول لوجوده فمحال إذا قولكم إن ما وقع عليه قولكم حوادث لا أول له أو منه ما لا أول له ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك
And if they say: there is no end to the distinctness of the two principles and their mixing, and even if they are originated, there is no distinctness except preceded by mixing, and no mixing except preceded by distinctness, forever with no beginning for that and no end, it is said to them: then according to your statement it is impossible that the world did not exist in pre-eternity, because mixing according to this statement of yours has no beginning, and nothing of it exists except that before it there was its like and a distinctness that differs from it without end. Then it is said to them and to whoever among the materialists (ahl al-dahr) says that: your statement that there is no beginning for originated things necessitates their pre-eternity and that they did not come into being from non-existence, and your statement about them that they are originated is a contradiction of that, because the word 'originated things' is a plural of 'originated', and the reality of the originated is that it is from non-existence. So the reality of the plural to which the name applies is that it exists from non-existence. It is impossible for something that has no beginning for its existence to enter into the plural of originated things. Therefore your statement is impossible: that what you call 'originated things' has no beginning, or that some of them have no beginning. And they have no answer for that.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
The final passage addresses two related claims. First, the dualists might posit an infinite regress of distinctness and mixing without beginning or end. The author points out that if mixing has no beginning, then in pre-eternity mixing must have already occurred, implying the world existed eternally—which contradicts the temporal existence of the world. Second, the author refutes the general materialist claim that originated things (al-hawadith) have no beginning. He argues that the very term 'originated' means 'came from non-existence'. If something has no beginning of existence, it cannot be called 'originated'. Therefore, calling events 'originated' while denying a beginning is self-contradictory. The dualists and materialists have no valid response to this logical refutation.

Key Terms
الحوادث
العدم
القدم
الأزل
أهل الدهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'originated' simply means 'happened in time' and could still apply to something without a beginning.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'originated' (hadith) linguistically and conceptually requires coming from non-existence, thus a beginning is intrinsic to its meaning.

Checkpoint
Why is it impossible for originated things to have no beginning?
A) Because everything has a beginning.
B) Because 'originated' means 'from non-existence'.
C) Because God creates everything.
D) Because the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The very term 'originated' (hadith) requires a beginning from non-existence, so events cannot both be originated and have no beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that 'originated' means from non-existence. Your very existence comes from nothing. Do you live each day as if you are a fresh creation from Allah, or do you behave as if you are self-sufficient and without a beginning?
?
The materialists claimed events have no beginning. When you delay repentance, do you treat your sins as if they have no beginning—as if they are just a natural part of you? How does knowing that every sin originates from a choice call you to cut it off at its root?
?
If you truly accept that you are originated from non-existence, then every moment is a gift. Is your gratitude to Allah evident in how you use your time? Or do you act as if your existence is owed to you?

Baqillani's Dilemma on Separation and Mixture of the Two Principles
Baqillani refutes dualist claim with a dilemma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses a dilemma argument to refute the dualist claim that the two principles are eternal but their separation and mixture are created.

Core Idea
If something is inseparable from created qualities, it must itself be created.

Baqillani confronts a dualist claim that the two eternal principles (light and darkness) have a created separation and mixture. He presents a simple but powerful dilemma that forces the dualist into a contradiction or an admission that the principles themselves are created. Let's see how the argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Notice the structure of the argument: a question followed by two possible responses.
Pay attention to the key terms: الأصلان, التباين, الامتزاج, المحدث.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
The question is: Can the two principles ever be free from separation and mixture? The first horn: if yes, then dualists abandon their claim of eternity. The second horn: if no, then the principles are inseparable from created qualities, so they must be created.
The dilemma is exhaustive; there is no third option.
3
Understand the logical conclusion
Baqillani applies a general principle: anything that never becomes free from the created and does not precede it is itself created. Since the principles cannot be free from created separation/mixture, they are created.
This principle is a bedrock of kalam arguments for the temporality of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا إن تباين الأصلين محدث وامتزاجهما محدث، قيل لهم : فهل ينفك الأصلان من التباين والامتزاج ؟ فإن قالوا: نعم، تركوا قولهم بتباين الأصلين في القدم وإن قالوا : لا، قيل لهم فيجب القضاء على حدوث النور والظلام إذا كانا لا ينفكان من حادثين ولا يخلوان منهما ; وكنا قد بينا أن ما لم ينفك من المحدّث ولم يسبقه فهو محدث
And if they say: The separation of the two principles is originated and their mixture is originated, it is said to them: Do the two principles ever become free from separation and mixture? If they say: Yes, they have abandoned their statement that the two principles are eternal in pre-eternity; and if they say: No, it is said to them: Then it is necessary to judge that light and darkness are originated, since they are never free from two originated things and are never devoid of them. And we have already explained that that which never becomes free from the originated and does not precede it is itself originated.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualist claim that the two principles (al-aṣlān) are eternal but their separation and mixture are created. Baqillani presents a dilemma: either the two principles can be free from separation and mixture, or they cannot. If they can be free, then the dualists give up their claim that the principles are eternal (since if they are ever without separation and mixture, then those qualities are not essential, but the dualists hold that the principles are eternally separate and mixed). If they cannot be free, then because separation and mixture are created, the principles are never devoid of created qualities, so they themselves must be created. This is a classic kalam argument that if something is inseparable from attributes that are known to be temporally originated, then that thing itself is temporally originated.

Key Terms
الأصلان (al-aṣlān)
التباين (al-tabāyun)
الامتزاج (al-imtizāj)
المحدث (al-muḥdath)
القدم (al-qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Something eternal can have created attributes without being created.
Correction: Kalam holds that if something is inseparable from created attributes, then it must itself be created, because it depends on them.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Baqillani draw if the two principles cannot be free from separation and mixture?
A) They are eternal
B) They are originated
C) They are neither
D) They are both

Key Takeaway
Baqillani uses a dilemma to show that the two principles cannot be eternal if their separation and mixture are created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Baqillani argues that if something is never free from originated qualities, it must itself be originated. Consider your own life: are you ever truly free from change, time, and decay? If not, what does that mean for your own nature and your need for the Eternal Creator?
?
The dualists claimed two eternal principles, but Baqillani forced them to confront the implications of their belief. Are there any beliefs you hold that you avoid examining logically because you fear the conclusion? Will you seek the truth even if it dismantles your assumptions?
?
If everything that is never free from the created is itself created, then every part of this world points to its Creator. How does this reality change the way you view the moments of your day—your thoughts, feelings, and actions? Will you let this truth transform your awareness of Allah’s presence?

Baqillani's Refutation of Infinite Regress
Baqillani disproves infinite regress of mixture and separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Baqillani's argument that an infinite regress of mixture and separation would require the world to be eternal, contradicting its temporality.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of mixture makes the world eternal.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani tackles a dualist claim that the processes of mixture and separation have no beginning, even though the two principles are originated. He uses a logical reductio: if mixture has no start, then the world it produces must also be eternal. This directly contradicts the dualists' own assertion that the world is temporally created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation without analyzing deeply. Let the objection and response stand out.
Notice the dualists' conditional: 'if they say...'
2
Identify the dualist claim
The dualists assert that mixture and separation have no beginning or end, despite being originated. This is a claim of infinite regress in temporal events.
3
Analyze Baqillani's reply
Baqillani points out that if mixture has no beginning, then the world (which results from mixture) would also have no beginning, making it eternal. This contradicts the world's createdness.
This is a reductio ad absurdum argument.
4
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider why an infinite regress of past events is impossible for a created world. This passage shows the necessary connection between beginningless mixture and an eternal world.
The key insight: a beginningless process cannot produce a temporal product.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قالوا لا غاية لتباين الأصلين وامتزاجهما، وإن كانا حادثين، ولا تباين إلا وقبله امتزاج، ولا امتزاج إلا وقبله تباين أبداً لا أول لذلك ولا غاية، قيل لهم : فمحال على قولكم هذا أن الدنيا لم تكن في الأزل; لأن الامتزاج على قولكم هذا لا أول له ولا شيء منه إلا وقد كان قبله مثله وتباين يخالفه إلى غير غاية
And if they say: There is no end to the separation of the two principles and their mixture, even if they are originated, and there is no separation except before it was a mixture, and no mixture except before it was a separation, forever, having no beginning and no end, it is said to them: According to this statement of yours, it is impossible that the world was not in pre-eternity; because mixture according to this statement has no beginning, and no part of it except that before it was something like it and its opposite separation, without any end.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage addresses the dualist objection that separation and mixture have an infinite regress, yet they still claim both principles are originated. Baqillani points out that if mixture has no beginning, then the world (the product of mixture) would have existed eternally, contradicting the claim that the world is temporally originated. The infinite regress of mixture and separation would entail that the world never began, which is impossible for a created world.

Key Terms
الغاية (al-ghāya)
التباين (al-tabāyun)
الامتزاج (al-imtizāj)
الأزل (al-azal)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Infinite regress of past events is possible for a created world.
Correction: Baqillani shows that if mixture has no beginning, then the world would be eternal, but since the world is created, an infinite regress is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what would it mean if mixture has no beginning?
A) The world is eternal
B) The world is created
C) The principles are eternal
D) None

Key Takeaway
An infinite regress of mixture and separation would make the world eternal, which contradicts its temporality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever held a belief simply because it seemed convenient, without examining whether it leads to a logical contradiction? What assumption in your life might be undermining the truth about your Creator today?
?
If the world has a beginning—and this argument shows infinite regress is impossible—then it has a Creator. Have you lived today as if that Creator is real, watching every thought and action? What will you do differently tomorrow to align your life with that reality?
?
Baqillani's argument forces the dualists to confront the consequences of their premise. Are there premises in your own worldview that, if followed to their logical end, would lead to something you know is false? Will you have the courage to abandon them, no matter how comfortable they seem?

The Definition of Events Refutes Eternal Occurrence
Baqillani proves that 'ḥawādith' by definition originate from non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the definition of 'ḥādith' (event) logically implies a beginning and refutes the materialist claim of an eternal series of events.

Core Idea
If something is an 'event', it must have come from non-existence—so it cannot be eternal.

Imam al-Baqillani presents a crisp linguistic and logical argument against materialists who claim the world is eternal yet composed of events. He points out a contradiction in their own terminology: the very word 'event' implies origination from non-existence. This short but powerful argument shows how careful attention to definitions can dismantle a philosophical position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice how Baqillani uses the definition of 'ḥādith' as his starting point.
The argument hinges on the meaning of the word, not on empirical observation.
2
Identify the key definition
Find where Baqillani states the real meaning (ḥaqīqa) of 'ḥādith'. How does he define it?
The definition is: 'that which comes from non-existence' (mā ʿan ʿadam).
3
Trace the logical contradiction
Follow how Baqillani shows that applying 'ḥawādith' to things without beginning is impossible. Why is it impossible?
Because if something has no beginning, it cannot have come from non-existence.
4
Reflect on the force of the argument
Consider why Baqillani says 'they have no answer for that'. What makes this argument so decisive?
The argument is based on the very language the opponents use—they cannot escape the definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم ولمن قال بذلك من أهل الدهر: إن قولكم لا أول للحوادث يقتضي قدمها وأنها لم تكن عن عدم ; وقولكم فيها إنها حوادث نقض لذلك; لأن القول «حوادث» هو جمع حادث; والحادث حقيقته أنه ما عن عدم ; فحقيقة الجمع الذي يقع عليه الاسم أنه موجود عن عدم . المحال أن يدخل في جمع الحوادث ما لا أول لوجوده، فمحال إذاً قولكم إن ما وقع عليه قولكم «حوادث لا أول له أو منه ما لا أول له ; ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك
Then it is said to them and to those who say that among the materialists: Your statement that there is no beginning for events implies their pre-eternity and that they were not from non-existence; and your statement that they are events contradicts that; because the word 'events' is the plural of event; and the real meaning of event is that which comes from non-existence; so the real meaning of the plural to which the name applies is that it exists after non-existence. It is impossible to include in the plural 'events' that which has no beginning for its existence. Therefore your statement that that to which your word 'events' applies has no beginning, or that some of it has no beginning, is impossible. And they have no answer for that.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and logical argument against the materialists (ahl al-dahr) who claim that the world is eternal yet composed of events (ḥawādith). Baqillani points out a contradiction: the term 'ḥawādith' (events, originated things) by definition means things that come into existence after non-existence. If they say the events have no beginning, then they would be eternal, which contradicts the meaning of 'ḥādith'. So it is impossible for something that is eternal to also be originated. This refutes their claim that the world is both eternal and composed of originated events.

Key Terms
الحوادث (al-ḥawādith)
الحادث (al-ḥādith)
القدم (al-qidam)
العدم (al-ʿadam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some materialists think that events (ḥawādith) can be eternal, i.e., have no beginning in time.
Correction: Baqillani shows that by definition an 'event' is something that comes from non-existence, so it must have a beginning. Eternal events are a contradiction in terms.

Checkpoint
According to Baqillani, what is the real meaning (ḥaqīqa) of 'ḥādith'?
A) That which has no beginning
B) That which exists from non-existence
C) That which is created by God
D) That which is temporal

Key Takeaway
The term 'event' (ḥādith) by definition implies origination from non-existence, so no event can be eternal without contradicting its own meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you been living as if something created (like your health, status, or possessions) is eternal, even though you know it is not? How can Baqillani's clear distinction between 'event' and 'eternal' change how you value that thing starting today?
?
Are there beliefs you hold that contradict the very definitions you claim to accept—for example, saying 'this world is temporary' yet devoting all your energy to it? What will you do this week to bring your actions in line with your stated belief about the nature of worldly things?

Refutation of the Dayṣāniyya: Darkness is Created
Baqillani refutes the Dayṣāniyya by turning their own reasoning against them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the dualists' own logic to prove that darkness is not eternal but created.

Core Idea
Internal consistency defeats dualism: if light is eternal, darkness cannot be.

The Dayṣāniyya claimed that darkness is a lifeless, evil principle coexisting with light. Baqillani challenges them with a simple logical turn: if light is eternal by itself, then by their own reasoning, darkness cannot be eternal. This passage shows how he exposes the inconsistency in their dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument come through.
Notice the concise, dialectical style of Baqillani.
2
Identify the dualist claim and their reasoning
The Dayṣāniyya claim that darkness is lifeless and evil because it is the opposite of light, which is alive. They think this opposition prevents darkness from being alive.
Their premise is that opposites cannot share attributes.
3
Observe Baqillani's logical turn
Baqillani points out that if light is eternal by itself, then by the same logic, darkness cannot be eternal—it must be originated. He turns their own reasoning against them.
This is a classic refutation by internal contradiction.
4
Understand the conclusion
The dualists cannot answer this. They must admit that darkness is created, which undermines their dualistic eternal principles.
The argument shows that reason alone can refute false doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في الرد على الديصانية. ويقال للديصانية منهم : لم زعمتم أن الظلام موات فعّال للشر بطبعه دون النور؟ فإن قالوا لأنهما لما كانا خلافين بأنفسهما وكان النور حياً بذاته، استحال أن يكون الظلام حياً بذاته يقال لهم : ما أنكرتم أن يكون الظلام محدثاً؛ لأنه لما ثبت من قولكم إن النور قديم لذاته استحال أن يكون الظلام قديماً بذاته ونفسه، ولا جواب لهم عن ذلك

Because since it is established from your statement that light is eternal by itself, it is impossible that darkness is eternal by itself and in its essence. And they have no answer for that.

Commentary
This passage refutes the Dayṣāniyya sect who claim that darkness is lifeless and evil by nature while light is alive and good. They argue that since light is alive, darkness cannot be alive. Baqillani turns their own reasoning against them: if light is eternal (qadīm) by itself, then by the same logic, darkness cannot be eternal by itself; it must be originated. This refutes their claim that both light and darkness are eternal principles.

The Dayṣāniyya Sect: Beliefs and Origins
Editor describes beliefs and origin of the Dayṣāniyya sect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the beliefs of the Dayṣāniyya sect, its origin, and how it differs from Manichaeism.

Core Idea
The Dayṣāniyya believed in two principles but differed from Manichaeism on how light and darkness mixed.

In this lesson, we explore the Dayṣāniyya sect, a dualist group that emerged before Manichaeism. The passage from Baqillani's work describes their name, their internal divisions, and their key doctrines. Understanding this sect helps us see the variety of dualist beliefs in early Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the origin of the sect
Read the passage to learn why the sect is called Dayṣāniyya and who its founder was.
The name comes from a river called Dayṣān.
2
Note the relation to Manichaeism
Observe that Dayṣān was before Mani, and the two schools are close but differ on the mixing of light and darkness.
3
Examine internal divisions
The passage mentions two sects within the Dayṣāniyya: one says light mixed voluntarily, the other says involuntarily.
This is the main difference among them.
4
Understand the nature of light and darkness
Ibn Dayṣān said light is one genus; some said darkness is the origin of light. Light is alive and knowing, darkness is inert.
5
Reflect on spread and writings
Note the geographical regions and the books attributed to Ibn Dayṣān and his followers.
They had no central congregation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الديصانية : هم أتباع ديصان، وإنما سمي صاحبهم بديصان باسم نهر ولد عليه. وهو قبل ما في صاحب المانوية. والمذهبان قريب بعضهم من بعض، وإنما بينهما خلف في اختلاط النور بالمظلمة فإن الديصانية اختلفت في ذلك على فرقتين ; فرقة زعمت أن النور خالط الظلمة باختيار منه، ليصلحها فلما حصل فيها ورام الخروج عنها، امتنع ذلك عليه. وفرقة زعمت أن النور أراد أن يرفع الظلمة عنه لما أحس بخشونتها ونتنها شابكها بغير اختيار، وزعم ابن ديصان ان النور جنس واحد وزعم بعض الديصانية، أن الظلمة أصل النور وذكر أن النور حي ، حساس، عالم، وأن الظلمة بضد ذلك عامية غير حساسة، ولا عالمة، فتكارها . وأصحاب ابن ديصان بنواحي البطائح كانوا قديماً، وبالصين وخراسان أمم منهم متفرقون. لا يعرف لهم جمع ولا بيعة ولابن ديصان كتاب النور والظلمة كتاب روحانية الحق. كتاب المتحرك والجماد ، وله كتب كثيرة ولرؤساء المذهب في ذلك أيضاً كتباً.
The Dayṣāniyya: they are followers of Dayṣān, and their founder was named Dayṣān after the name of a river on which he was born. He was before the founder of Manichaeism. The two schools are close to each other; the difference between them is concerning the mixing of light with darkness. The Dayṣāniyya differed on that into two sects: a sect claimed that light mixed with darkness by its choice to reform it, but when it got into it and sought to exit, it was impossible; and a sect claimed that light wanted to remove darkness from itself when it felt its roughness and stench, so it entangled with it involuntarily. Ibn Dayṣān claimed that light is one genus. Some Dayṣāniyya claimed that darkness is the origin of light and mentioned that light is alive, sensing, knowing, and darkness is contrary to that: inert, non-sensing, and non-knowing. The followers of Ibn Dayṣān were in the regions of al-Baṭāʾiḥ in ancient times, and in China and Khurāsān there were scattered nations of them. They have no known congregation or place of worship. Ibn Dayṣān wrote the Book of Light and Darkness, the Book of the Spirituality of Truth, the Book of the Moving and the Inanimate, and he has many other books; also the leaders of the school have books on that.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an editorial note providing background on the Dayṣāniyya sect, a dualist group akin to Manichaeism. It explains the origin of the name, the differences among them regarding the mixing of light and darkness (some say voluntary, others involuntary), and their beliefs about the natures of light and darkness. It also mentions their founder, writings, and geographical spread. This footnote clarifies the sect being refuted in the previous argument.

ديصان (Dayṣān)

Misconception: The Dayṣāniyya are the same as Manichaeism.
Correction: They differ in how light and darkness mixed; the Dayṣāniyya had two views on that, while Manichaeism had its own distinct view.

Refuting Dualists: The Argument from Mixing
Baqillani argues that if eternal beings can mix, they can also transform into each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani uses the principle of consistency to refute the dualist claim that eternal light and darkness can mix without transforming into each other.

Core Idea
If eternal beings can mix, they can also transform into each other.

Baqillani presents a concise logical argument against all dualist sects who posit two eternal principles, light and darkness. He challenges the consistency of their position: if they admit that these principles can change from separate to mixed, why not also from light to darkness? This simple question undermines their entire worldview.

Guided Path
1
Understand the dualist position
Recall that dualists (Thunawiyya) believe in two eternal principles: light and darkness. They argue that these can mix but not transform into each other.
This sets up the background for Baqillani's refutation.
2
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Notice the brevity and directness of the argument.
The passage is a single question that encapsulates a powerful logical point.
3
Analyze the logical structure
Identify the conditional: 'If it is permissible for separate things to become mixed, then why not for light to become darkness?' This is an argument from analogy or consistency.
The dualists already accept one change (mixing); the argument forces them to accept another (transformation) by the same logic.
4
Consider the dualists' dilemma
Reflect on why the dualists cannot answer. They either admit transformation (absurd) or deny mixing (contradicting their own view). Either way, their position is untenable.
This shows the strength of Baqillani's argument: it leaves no escape.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في الرد على جميع الثنوية. ويقال لجميعهم : إذا جاز أن يصير ما لم يزل متبايناً ممتزجاً فلم لا يجوز أن يصير ما لم يزل نوراً ظلاماً وما لم يزل ظلاماً نوراً? فلا يجدون له مدفعاً.
A question concerning the refutation of all dualists. And it is said to all of them: If it is permissible for that which has not ceased to be separate to become mixed, then why is it not permissible for that which has not ceased to be light to become darkness, and that which has not ceased to be darkness to become light? They will find no way to repel this.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is a brief but powerful argument against all dualist sects. If the dualists admit that the two eternal principles (light and darkness) can change from being separate to being mixed, then by the same logic, they could also change their very natures—light becoming darkness and vice versa. This leads to an absurdity that they cannot answer.

Key Terms
الثنوية (al-Thunawiyya)
التباين (al-tabāyun)
الامتزاج (al-imtizāj)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists believe that eternal light and darkness can mix without changing their essential natures (i.e., light remains light, darkness remains darkness).
Correction: Baqillani argues that if mixing is a change from separation, then transformation is also a change, and there is no rational basis to allow one and forbid the other.

Checkpoint
What does Baqillani argue against the dualists?
A) If light and darkness can mix, they can also transform into each other.
B) Light and darkness cannot mix because they are opposite.
C) The world is eternal because light and darkness have always existed.
D) Dualism is false because there is only one eternal principle.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's argument shows that admitting any change in eternal principles leads to absurdity, thereby undermining dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you admit that you can change small habits for the worse, why do you resist the complete transformation that Allah calls you to? What 'darkness' in your life are you claiming is too permanent to change?
?
Dualism allows mixing but rejects transformation. Where in your own heart do you accept a mixture of obedience and disobedience, yet deny that you need a total transformation to Allah's light? What step can you take today to abandon that dual allegiance?

Necessitating Dualists on the Claimant of Darkness
Baqillani forces dualists to classify a claimant to darkness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani forces dualists into a rational dilemma by asking about the classification of a claimant to darkness.

Core Idea
If a being of light claims to be darkness, light itself would be lying.

Baqillani continues his refutation of dualists who believe in two eternal principles, light and darkness. He presents a clever argument that forces them to classify any claimant. This passage shows the first step of the dilemma.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own questioning style come through.
Notice that Baqillani uses a direct question to the dualists.
2
Identify the dilemma's structure
The dualist is asked to classify a person who says 'I am darkness.' They must choose from individuals of light or darkness. The passage explores the consequence if they choose light.
The other option (darkness) is implied but not spelled out.
3
Understand the ilzām (necessitation)
If they say the claimant is from light, then light is attributed with a false claim—since light cannot become darkness. This forces an absurdity, showing the dualist stance is irrational.
This argument does not rely on revealed sources but pure reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في الزام جميع الثنوية. ويقال لجميعهم أيضاً : خبرونا عن قائل قال : أنا ظلام هل يخلو أن يكون من أشخاص النور أو من أشخاص الظلام? فإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم : فمن أيهما هو? فإن قالوا من أشخاص النور، قيل لهم : فقد كذب النور إذاً
A question concerning the necessitation on all dualists. And it is said to all of them also: Inform us about a speaker who says: 'I am darkness.' Is he free from being from the individuals of light or from the individuals of darkness? If they say: No, it is said to them: Then from which of them is he? If they say: From the individuals of light, it is said to them: Then light has lied if so.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents a dilemma for dualists. If someone claims to be darkness, they must categorize him. If they say he is from the individuals of light, then light would be lying by claiming to be darkness. If they say he is from the individuals of darkness, then he is darkness. The argument forces them to admit that such a claim is impossible, thereby showing the absurdity of their belief that light and darkness are distinct substances.

Key Terms
الزام (ilzām)
أشخاص (ashkhāṣ)
النور (al-nūr)
الظلام (al-ẓulām)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Dualists might think a being of light can truthfully claim to be darkness.
Correction: If light is true and cannot become darkness, then claiming to be darkness would be a lie, contradicting the nature of light.

Checkpoint
What does the argument show if the dualists say the claimant is from light?
A) Light is truthful
B) Light has lied
C) The claimant is from darkness
D) The claim is impossible

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's dilemma demonstrates that dualists cannot consistently classify a claimant to darkness without contradiction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If light itself were to claim darkness, it would be lying. Are there areas where you are claiming virtues you do not possess, hiding in the shadow of hypocrisy? What step will you take this week to align your speech with your true state?

Refutation of Dualists: The Mixing Argument
Baqillani refutes Dualists with a reductio on mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a simple logical argument from mixing refutes the Dualist claim that light and darkness are eternal and distinct.

Core Idea
If eternal things can mix, they can change into each other.

This lesson presents Imam al-Baqillani's concise refutation of all Dualists. The argument uses the Dualists' own acceptance of mixing to show the absurdity of their position. By following this logical move, you will see how a single question can dismantle an entire worldview.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Start by reading the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the brevity and force of the argument.
The passage uses key terms like 'ما لم يزل' (eternal) and 'ممتزج' (mixed).
2
Understand the Dualist claim
Dualists believe in two eternal principles: light and darkness. They are distinct and not created. Baqillani addresses this claim.
The argument does not require knowledge of Dualist details; it works on any two eternal distinct entities.
3
Grasp the logical move
Baqillani notes that Dualists accept mixing of light and darkness. He then asks: if mixing is possible, why can't light become darkness and vice versa? This exposes a contradiction.
Mixing requires some commonality or change, which implies potential for transformation.
4
See the force of the argument
If eternal light can become darkness, then it is not eternally light. Similarly for darkness. Thus the Dualist position collapses.
The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: their own premise leads to an absurd conclusion.
5
Reflect on why it works
The argument works because it challenges the core Dualist assumption that eternal principles are immutable. Mixing implies changeability.
This shows the power of logical reasoning in theological debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في الرد على جميع الثنوية. ويقال لجميعهم: إذا جاز أن يصير ما لم يزل متبايناً ممتزجاً فلِمَ لا يجوز أن يصير ما لم يزل نوراً ظلاماً وما لم يزل ظلاماً نوراً؟ فلا يجدون له مدفعاً.
Issue concerning the refutation of all Dualists. And it is said to all of them: If it is possible for that which has not ceased to be (the eternal) to become [from] distinct to mixed, then why is it not possible for that which has not ceased to be (light) to become darkness, and that which has not ceased to be (darkness) to become light? So they will find no dodge for it.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This is a short argument against all Dualists. It uses a reductio: if the eternal (light and darkness) can mix after being distinct, then why cannot the eternal light become darkness and vice versa? This would mean the substances are changeable, which contradicts their eternity. They cannot refute this.

Key Terms
الثنوية
ما لم يزل
متباين
ممتزج
نور
ظلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mixing does not imply the potential for transformation of essence.
Correction: The argument shows that mixing itself is a change in state. If an eternal entity can undergo one type of change (mixing), then it is changeable in principle, and there is no logical barrier to other types of change like transformation. Thus the Dualist cannot consistently allow mixing while denying transformation.

Checkpoint
What logical consequence does the argument draw from the Dualists' acceptance of mixing?
A) That light and darkness must be one substance
B) That light could become darkness and vice versa
C) That mixing is impossible
D) That the eternal cannot change

Key Takeaway
The Dualists' acceptance of mixing leads to the absurd conclusion that eternal light can become darkness, refuting their claim of eternal distinct principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The Dualists allowed mixing but not transformation—where in your life do you allow exceptions that could undermine core principles? Can you remove them now?

Obliging all Dualists: First Argument
Forcing Dualists to accept opposites from one substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani forces Dualists to admit that truth and falsehood can originate from a single substance, thereby undermining their dualistic framework.

Core Idea
A single substance can produce opposites, refuting dualism.

In this passage, Imam Baqillani presents a powerful logical argument against Dualists, who believe in two separate eternal substances—light and darkness—as sources of all opposites. Using a simple dilemma about a speaker claiming 'I am darkness,' he shows that truth and falsehood must both come from one substance, contradicting their fundamental premise. This argument effectively collapses the Dualist's distinction between good and evil, justice and injustice, into one principle.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Dualist position
Dualists believe in two independent substances (light and darkness) that produce opposites: light with good, truth, etc.; darkness with evil, falsehood, etc. Recap this background to appreciate the force of the argument.
2
Follow Baqillani's dilemma
He asks about a speaker who says 'I am darkness.' If the speaker is from light, he lies (falsehood from light); if from darkness, he tells truth (truth from darkness). Either way, truth and falsehood issue from a single substance—the one the speaker belongs to.
Notice how the argument forces the Dualist to choose an answer, but both choices lead to the same conclusion.
3
Recognize the consequence
If truth and falsehood can come from one substance, then by extension, other opposites like good/evil, justice/injustice, cooling/heating can also come from one substance. This collapses the Dualist's two-substance system.
4
Reflect on the argument's strength
The argument is decisive because it forces an admission from the same data the Dualists accept. It does not rely on external premises, only on the Dualist's own categories.
This is a classic 'obliging' argument (ilzam) in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة في الزام جميع الثنوية. ويُقالُ لجميعهم أيضاً: خبرونا عن قائل قال: أنا ظلام هل يخلو أن يكون من أشخاص النور أو من أشخاص الظلام؟ فإن قالوا: لا، قيل لهم: فمن أيهما هو؟ فإن قالوا من أشخاص النور، قيل لهم: فقد كَذَبَ النور إذاً في قوله «أنا ظلام» لأنه ليس بظلام وهذا نقض قولكم؛ وإن قالوا: من أشخاص الظلام، قيل لهم: فقد صدق في قوله «أنا ظلامٌ» ووُجد الصدقُ والكذب من جوهر واحد وإن جاز ذلك جاز وقوع الخير والشر والعدل والجور والتبريد والتسخين من جوهر واحد وهذا ترك دينهم.
Issue concerning the obliging of all Dualists. And it is said to all of them also: Inform us about a speaker who said: 'I am darkness'—is it free from being either from the individuals of light or from the individuals of darkness? For if they say: No, it is said to them: Then from which of them is he? For if they say from the individuals of light, it is said to them: Then light has lied in its statement 'I am darkness' because it is not darkness, and this is a refutation of your statement. And if they say: from the individuals of darkness, it is said to them: Then it has told the truth in its statement 'I am darkness,' and truth and falsehood are found from a single substance. And if that is permissible, then the occurrence of good and evil, justice and injustice, cooling and heating from a single substance is permissible, and this is an abandonment of their religion.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage presents an argument that forces the Dualists to accept that truth and falsehood can come from one substance, undermining their claim of two separate substances. By asking about a person claiming 'I am darkness,' if he is from light, he lies; if from darkness, he tells truth. Thus truth and falsehood proceed from one substance (darkness), and by extension good and evil, justice and injustice, etc., can all come from one substance, which contradicts their dualism.

Key Terms
الزام
الثنوية
أشخاص النور
أشخاص الظلام
صدق
كذب
جوهر
خير
شر
عدل
جور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Truth and falsehood cannot proceed from the same substance because they are opposites and opposites require separate sources.
Correction: Baqillani's dilemma shows that in either case (speaker from light or darkness), truth and falsehood both arise from a single substance, proving that opposites can emanate from one source.

Checkpoint
What is the immediate effect of the argument about the speaker claiming 'I am darkness'?
A) It proves that light and darkness are eternal
B) It shows that truth and falsehood can come from one substance
C) It demonstrates that darkness cannot speak
D) It forces the Dualist to deny that mixing occurs

Key Takeaway
Baqillani shows that if a Dualist admits any speaker saying 'I am darkness,' they must concede that opposites can emanate from one substance, defeating dualism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to be something you are not—a 'darkness' in character—are you aware that the same 'I' also produces truth? How does this lesson challenge you to take responsibility for both your virtues and your vices as coming from your single self?
?
Baqillani forces the Dualist to admit that opposites can come from one source. Do you ever separate your 'good' actions from your 'bad' actions as if they originate from different parts of you, rather than owning them all as your own? What would change if you stopped doing that?
?
If the existence of opposites does not prove two different creators, then where do you personally tend to place the blame for evil? How can this argument reshape your view of divine justice and your own accountability?

Refuting the Dualist on Evil from Darkness
Refutes the claim that truth and falsehood are both evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical kalam refutes a Dualist objection by exposing an inconsistency in their moral reasoning.

Core Idea
Consistency forces Dualists to either accept all actions as evil or none.

The Dualist argues that both truth and falsehood are evil, so darkness is purely evil. The refutation challenges this by asking why justice and pleasure cannot also be considered evil, and if they refuse that, they must also refuse that truth is evil. This exposes a contradiction in their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: first the Dualist's objection, then the refutation's response.
2
Identify the Dualist's claim and the refutation strategy
The Dualist claims truth and falsehood from darkness are both evil. The refutation responds by asking: if that is so, why cannot justice and injustice, pleasure and pain also be evil? If they object that justice and pleasure cannot be evil, then truth cannot be evil either.
Focus on the logical move: forcing consistency.
3
Reflect on the effectiveness
Ask yourself why this argument is effective. It forces the Dualist to choose between accepting that all actions from darkness are evil (including justice) or admitting that truth is not evil. Either way, their position becomes untenable.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قال منهم قائل: قد وقع الصدق والكذب من جوهر الظلام وهما شران، قيل لهم: ما أنكرتم من أن يقع منه الجور والعدل والإيلامُ والإِلْذَاذُ ويكون شرّاً كلّه؟ فإن قالوا: لا يجوز أن يكون من العدل والإنذاذ شر، قيل لهم: ولا يجوز أن يكون من الصدق شر.
For if a speaker among them says: Indeed truth and falsehood have occurred from the substance of darkness, and both are evils, it is said to them: What prevents you from acknowledging that injustice and justice and causing pain and causing pleasure occur from it, and all of it is evil? For if they say: It is not permissible for justice and pleasure to be evil, it is said to them: And it is not permissible for truth to be evil.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
Here the Dualist objects that truth and falsehood from darkness are both evil, so this does not undermine their claim that only evil comes from darkness. The response: if you say that, then why not also accept that justice and injustice, pleasure and pain, can all come from darkness and be evil? But if they say justice and pleasure cannot be evil, then truth cannot be evil either. So the objection fails.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that if all actions from a substance are of one moral value (evil), then the substance is purely evil.
Correction: The refutation shows that the Dualist's own framework would also require them to label justice and pleasure as evil, which they reject, so their assumption is inconsistent.

Checkpoint

A) It denies that truth and falsehood are evil
B) It accepts the premise and forces consistency about justice and injustice
C) It argues that darkness cannot produce truth
D) It asserts that justice is also evil

Key Takeaway
The refutation works by reducing the Dualist to a choice: either accept that all actions (justice, pleasure, etc.) from darkness are evil, or admit that truth cannot be evil—neither option supports their original claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accuse others of wrongdoing, do you apply the same standard to yourself? Consider how the Dualist's inconsistency mirrors your own double standards in judging others' actions versus your own.
?
Truth is often uncomfortable. When you encounter a truth that challenges your pride or convenience, do you try to label it as 'bad' or 'harmful' to avoid living by it? What truth are you resisting right now?
?
The refutation forces the Dualist to choose between accepting all actions as evil or admitting truth is good. In your life, what comfortable lie are you holding onto that you need to surrender in order to embrace the full weight of truth?

Truth and Falsehood from One Substance
al-Baqillani challenges dualists with a rhetorical analogy between truth/falsehood and justice/injustice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Baqillani uses the distinction between truth and falsehood to argue that opposite actions can proceed from a single substance.

Core Idea
Opposite effects do not require opposite causes.

In this argument, al-Baqillani presses the dualists further. He points out that even in their own system, truth and falsehood arise from the same principle (darkness) despite being opposites. So why reject that justice and injustice come from one divine substance? This rhetorical challenge forces them to either accept a single source or give up their premise.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it is a rhetorical question directed at dualists who claim light and darkness are two sources.
Focus on the logical structure: truth vs falsehood, then justice vs injustice.
2
Identify the parallel
Al-Baqillani draws a parallel between the pair truth/falsehood and the pair justice/injustice. Both pairs are opposites. If dualists accept that opposite reports (truth/falsehood) come from darkness, why reject that opposite actions (justice/injustice) come from one substance?
3
Understand the rhetorical strategy
This is a reductio ad absurdum: the dualists' own admission about truth and falsehood forces them to accept that justice and injustice can come from one source. If they deny the latter, they must also deny the former, which they cannot.
The argument does not prove that the one substance is Allah, but it removes the dualists' objection.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this argument is effective: it uses the dualists' own premises against them. It shows that difference in moral quality (good vs evil) does not require difference in origin.
This is a key point in Islamic theology against dualism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويقال لهم أيضاً: اعملوا على أن الصدق والكذب الواقعين من الظلام شرِّ أليس أحدُهما خبراً عن الشيء على ما هو به والآخر خبر عنه خلاف ما هو به؟ فما أنكرتم أن يقع العدل والجور من جوهر واحدٍ مع اختلافهما.
And it is said to them also: Reflect that truth and falsehood occurring from darkness are evil—is not one of them a report about a thing as it is, and the other a report about it contrary to what it is? So what prevents you from acknowledging that justice and injustice occur from a single substance despite their difference?
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This argument points out that truth and falsehood are different in kind: one reports reality, the other does not. If both are allowed from darkness, then why not allow justice and injustice from one substance, despite their difference? This forces further concession.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Opposite actions (good/evil) must come from opposite sources.
Correction: The argument shows that truth and falsehood are also opposites but both come from darkness, so the premise is false.

Checkpoint

B) One is a report according to reality, the other contrary to it
C) One is evil, the other is good
D) One requires knowledge, the other does not

Key Takeaway
One substance can produce opposite effects; the dualists' own admission about truth and falsehood proves this.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Daysaniyya Objection on Darkness and Truthfulness
Imam Baqillani refutes the Daysaniyya objection about darkness's truthfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Baqillani refutes the claim that truthfulness requires knowledge and intention, using the example of darkness's self-report.

Core Idea
Truth can exist without intention if the report matches reality.

The Daysaniyya, a dualist sect, argued that darkness cannot be truthful because it lacks knowledge and intention. Imam Baqillani responds by pointing out that the statement 'I am darkness' is still a report according to reality. Moreover, since darkness can also make false reports, it can produce both truth and falsehood, undermining the objection. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument. Note the structure: objection followed by response.
Pay attention to the rhetorical question 'Why do you say that?' which signals the refutation.
2
Understand the Daysaniyya objection
The Daysaniyya claim that darkness cannot be truthful because truth requires knowledge (علم) and intention (قصد). Without these, any statement from darkness is not considered truthful, even if it matches reality.
3
Analyze the refutation's logic
Follow Baqillani's response: first, he challenges the premise by asking why they hold that view. Then he argues that the statement 'I am darkness' is indeed a report about a thing as it is (خبر عن الشيء على ما هو به). Since darkness can also produce false reports, it can be a source of both truth and falsehood, and consequently both justice and injustice.
Notice how the refutation uses the same example to show that truth does not require knowledge.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider the broader theological point: truthfulness is defined by correspondence to reality, not by the agent's epistemic state. This defends the possibility of attributing actions to non-rational agents in theological debates.
This argument is part of a larger discussion on dualism and theodicy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن قال من الدِّيصانية قائل: إن الظلام ليس بصادق في قوله أنا ظلام؛ لأنه غير عالم بقوله وما كان منه والصدق مقرون بالقصد إليه والعلم به؛ قيل له: لم قلت ذلك؟ ثم يقال: أفليس هو مع ما وصفت خبر عن الشيء على ما هو به وقد يوجد أيضاً من الظلام الخبر عن الشيء على خلاف ما هو به فما أنكرت من جواز وقوع العدل والجور جميعاً من الظلام ولا فصل في ذلك.
And if a speaker from the Daysaniyya says: Indeed darkness is not truthful in its statement 'I am darkness' because it is not knowing of its statement and what comes from it, and truth is connected to intention toward it and knowledge of it; it is said to him: Why do you say that? Then it is said: Is it not, despite what you described, a report about a thing as it is? And there may also occur from darkness a report about a thing contrary to what it is. So what prevents you from acknowledging the permissibility of the occurrence of justice and injustice both from darkness, and there is no distinction in that.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
Another objection: darkness cannot be truthful because it lacks knowledge and intention. The response: even without knowledge, the statement 'I am darkness' is still a report according to reality (if the speaker is darkness). And since darkness can also make false reports, then it can produce both truth and falsehood. So it can also produce justice and injustice. There is no distinction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Assuming that truthfulness requires knowledge and intention in the agent, even when the statement accidentally corresponds to reality.
Correction: Truth is defined by correspondence to reality, not by the agent's knowledge or intention. A statement is true if it reports things as they are, regardless of the speaker's awareness.

Checkpoint

A) It denies that darkness can speak
B) It argues that the statement is still a report about reality
C) It claims that darkness does have knowledge
D) It says truth does not require correspondence

Key Takeaway
Truthfulness does not require knowledge and intention; a statement that matches reality is true regardless of the speaker's awareness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If truth does not require knowledge or intention, then how much of your own speech is truthful by accident? Will you now take greater responsibility to ensure you actually know what you say is true, rather than relying on correspondence alone?
?
This passage argues that even darkness can produce both truth and falsehood. What does that imply about your own capacity for both justice and injustice, regardless of your awareness? Are you willing to examine your actions for their actual correspondence to truth, even if you intended something else?

Refutation of Dualists on Truth and Falsehood from One Substance
Baqillani refutes dualist claims about truth/falsehood and good/evil from one substance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Baqillani refutes dualist claims by showing that if truth and falsehood can arise from one substance, then other opposites (good/evil, justice/injustice) must also arise from one substance, undermining their religion.

Core Idea
Consistency forces dualists to accept that one substance produces all opposites.

Baqillani engages with dualists who claim darkness cannot produce truth. He counters by exposing an inconsistency in their own position: if they admit that darkness can produce both truth and falsehood (opposites), then by the same logic it should produce good and evil, justice and injustice, etc. This passage demonstrates a classic reductio ad absurdum argument against dualism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the full English translation. Let the argument unfold naturally without over-analyzing.
The Arabic is dense; the translation helps clarify the logical steps.
2
Identify the dualist claim and Baqillani's first move
Notice the dualist statement 'I am darkness' and Baqillani's challenge that truth and falsehood come from one substance (darkness). This is the seed of his refutation.
3
Trace the logical extension to other opposites
Baqillani argues that if truth and falsehood come from one substance, then good/evil, justice/injustice, cooling/heating must also come from one substance. Understand why this is a problem for dualists.
This is the core analogy: if opposites come from one source, dualism collapses.
4
Analyze the objection and Baqillani's response
The dualist objects that truth and falsehood from darkness are both evil. Baqillani turns it around: if justice and pleasure cannot be evil, then truth cannot be evil either. This forces them to be consistent.
He also addresses the Manichaean claim that darkness lacks intention/knowledge for truth.
5
Reflect on the method
Consider how Baqillani uses the dualists' own premises to force a contradiction. This is a classic reductio argument.
The key is that he does not impose external standards but uses their own logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في قوله «أنا ظلام لأنه ليس بظلام وهذا نقض قولكم؛ وإن قالوا: من أشخاص الظلام، قيل لهم. فقد صدق في قوله «أنا ظلامٌ ووُجد الصدقُ والكذب من جوهر واحد وإن جاز ذلك جاز وقوع الخير والشر والعدل والجور والتبريد والتسخين من جوهر واحد وهذا ترك دينهم، فإن قال منهم قائل: قد وقع الصدق والكذب من جوهر الظلام وهما شران، قيل لهم: ما أنكرتم من أن يقع منه الجور والعدل والإيلامُ والإِلْذَاذُ ويكون شراً كلّه؟ فإن قالوا: لا يجوز أن يكون من العدل والإيلام شر، قيل لهم: ولا يجوز أن يكون من الصدق شر. ويقال لهم أيضاً: اعملوا على أن الصدق والكذب الواقعين من الظلام شرَّ أليس أحدُهما خبراً عن الشيء على ما هو به والآخر خبر عنه خلاف ما هو به؟ فما أنكرتم أن يقع العدل والجور من جوهر واحدٍ مع اختلافهما. وإن قال من الدِّيصانية قائل: إن الظلام ليس بصادق في قوله أنا ظلام؛ لأنه غير عالم بقوله وما كان منه والصدق مقرون بالقصد إليه والعلم به؛ قيل له: لم قلت ذلك؟ ثم يقال: أفليس هو مع ما وصفت خبراً عن الشيء على ما هو به وقد يوجد أيضاً من الظلام الخبر عن الشيء على خلاف ما هو به فما أنكرت من جواز وقوع العدل والجور جميعاً من الظلام ولا فصل في ذلك.
Regarding his statement, 'I am darkness because he is not darkness' — this is a refutation of your statement. And if they say: from the individual darknesses, it is said to them: He indeed spoke truthfully in his statement 'I am darkness,' and truth and falsehood have occurred from one substance. And if that is permitted, then the occurrence of good and evil, justice and injustice, cooling and heating from one substance is permitted, and this is an abandonment of their religion. So if one of them says: truth and falsehood have occurred from the substance of darkness, and both are evil, it is said to them: what prevents you from allowing that injustice and justice, causing pain and causing pleasure, occur from it and all be evil? If they say: it is not permissible for there to be evil from justice and pleasure, it is said to them: nor is it permissible for there to be evil from truth. And it is also said to them: consider that truth and falsehood occurring from darkness are evil. Is not one of them a report about a thing as it is, and the other a report about it contrary to what it is? So what prevents you from allowing that justice and injustice occur from one substance despite their difference? And if a speaker from the Manichaeans says: darkness is not truthful in its statement 'I am darkness' because it is not knowing of its statement and what comes from it, and truth is connected to intention and knowledge of it, it is said to him: why did you say that? Then it is said: is it not, despite what you described, a report about a thing as it is? And there also occurs from darkness a report about a thing contrary to what it is. So what prevents you from allowing the occurrence of both justice and injustice from darkness, with no distinction in that?
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the dualists (الثنوية) who claim that there are two principles, light and darkness. Baqillani begins by citing a dualist statement 'I am darkness because he (the speaker) is not darkness,' which the dualists consider false. He argues that if darkness can produce both truth and falsehood (since it stated 'I am darkness' and also falsehoods), and these opposites come from one substance, then by the same logic good and evil, justice and injustice, cooling and heating can also come from one substance. This would destroy their religion. He then addresses an objection that truth and falsehood from darkness are both evil, and responds by turning it around: if they say that justice and pleasure cannot be evil, then neither can truth be evil. He then argues that truth and falsehood are different in kind (one reports things as they are, the other contrary), yet the dualists allow both to come from darkness; thus they should allow justice and injustice from one substance as well. Finally, he refutes a Manichaean (دِصانية) objection that darkness cannot be truthful because it lacks intention and knowledge. Baqillani counters that darkness does produce reports both accurate and inaccurate, so the same principle applies. The argument follows a logical flow: accept opposite effects from one substance in one case, then consistency demands accepting them in all cases.

Key Terms
ظلام (darkness)
جوهر (substance)
صدق/كذب (truth/falsehood)
عدل/جور (justice/injustice)
خير/شر (good/evil)
دِصانية (Manichaeans)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Baqillani is granting that truth and falsehood are both evil.
Correction: He is actually using the dualists' own logic to force a contradiction: if they classify truth and falsehood from darkness as evil, then by their own standard justice and pleasure from darkness would also be evil, which they deny. The point is consistency, not agreement.

Checkpoint
What is the main logical move Baqillani makes against the dualists in this passage?
A) He proves that darkness is not a substance.
B) He argues that if they allow truth and falsehood from one substance, they must allow good and evil from one substance.
C) He denies that truth and falsehood can come from darkness.
D) He claims that the Manichaeans are not dualists.

Key Takeaway
Baqillani's refutation shows that logical consistency demands that if one opposite pair arises from a single substance, then all opposite pairs must, undermining the dualist separation into light and darkness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen that dualists were forced to accept that if one substance produces opposite effects, then all opposites must come from that substance. Now consider your own life: Where do you inconsistently allow one source to produce both good and evil, yet refuse to accept that the same source can produce other opposites? How will you rectify that inconsistency starting today?
?
Baqillani shows that the dualists' own argument collapses when pressed to its logical conclusion. When have you clung to a comfortable belief despite it contradicting a deeper truth you already hold? What is it costing you to avoid that logical conclusion?
?
The Manichaean objected that darkness cannot be truthful because it lacks intention. Yet Baqillani pointed out that it still produces accurate reports. Reflect: Do you sometimes excuse your own inconsistencies by claiming 'I didn't mean it' or 'I wasn't thinking'? How does this passage challenge you to take full responsibility for the consequences of your actions, even when unintentional?
